<a name="3.2.239">More than mine own; that am, have, and will be--</a><br>
<a name="3.2.240">Though all the world should crack their duty to you,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.241">And throw it from their soul; though perils did</a><br>
<a name="3.2.242">Abound, as thick as thought could make 'em, and</a><br>
<a name="3.2.243">Appear in forms more horrid,--yet my duty,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.244">As doth a rock against the chiding flood,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.245">Should the approach of this wild river break,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.246">And stand unshaken yours.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech64"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.247">'Tis nobly spoken:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.248">Take notice, lords, he has a loyal breast,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.249">For you have seen him open't. Read o'er this;</a><br>
<p><i>Giving him papers</i></p>
<a name="3.2.250">And after, this: and then to breakfast with</a><br>
<a name="3.2.251">What appetite you have.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit KING HENRY VIII, frowning upon CARDINAL WOLSEY: the Nobles throng after him, smiling and whispering</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech65"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.252">What should this mean?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.253">What sudden anger's this? how have I reap'd it?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.254">He parted frowning from me, as if ruin</a><br>
<a name="3.2.255">Leap'd from his eyes: so looks the chafed lion</a><br>
<a name="3.2.256">Upon the daring huntsman that has gall'd him;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.257">Then makes him nothing. I must read this paper;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.258">I fear, the story of his anger. 'Tis so;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.259">This paper has undone me: 'tis the account</a><br>
<a name="3.2.260">Of all that world of wealth I have drawn together</a><br>
<a name="3.2.261">For mine own ends; indeed, to gain the popedom,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.262">And fee my friends in Rome. O negligence!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.263">Fit for a fool to fall by: what cross devil</a><br>
<a name="3.2.264">Made me put this main secret in the packet</a><br>
<a name="3.2.265">I sent the king? Is there no way to cure this?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.266">No new device to beat this from his brains?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.267">I know 'twill stir him strongly; yet I know</a><br>
<a name="3.2.268">A way, if it take right, in spite of fortune</a><br>
<a name="3.2.269">Will bring me off again. What's this? 'To the Pope!'</a><br>
<a name="3.2.270">The letter, as I live, with all the business</a><br>
<a name="3.2.271">I writ to's holiness. Nay then, farewell!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.272">I have touch'd the highest point of all my greatness;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.273">And, from that full meridian of my glory,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.274">I haste now to my setting: I shall fall</a><br>
<a name="3.2.275">Like a bright exhalation m the evening,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.276">And no man see me more.</a><br>
<p><i>Re-enter to CARDINAL WOLSEY, NORFOLK and SUFFOLK, SURREY, and the Chamberlain</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech66"><b>NORFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.277">Hear the king's pleasure, cardinal: who commands you</a><br>
<a name="3.2.278">To render up the great seal presently</a><br>
<a name="3.2.279">Into our hands; and to confine yourself</a><br>
<a name="3.2.280">To Asher House, my Lord of Winchester's,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.281">Till you hear further from his highness.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech67"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.282">Stay:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.283">Where's your commission, lords? words cannot carry</a><br>
<a name="3.2.284">Authority so weighty.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech68"><b>SUFFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.285">Who dare cross 'em,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.286">Bearing the king's will from his mouth expressly?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech69"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.287">Till I find more than will or words to do it,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.288">I mean your malice, know, officious lords,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.289">I dare and must deny it. Now I feel</a><br>
<a name="3.2.290">Of what coarse metal ye are moulded, envy:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.291">How eagerly ye follow my disgraces,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.292">As if it fed ye! and how sleek and wanton</a><br>
<a name="3.2.293">Ye appear in every thing may bring my ruin!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.294">Follow your envious courses, men of malice;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.295">You have Christian warrant for 'em, and, no doubt,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.296">In time will find their fit rewards. That seal,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.297">You ask with such a violence, the king,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.298">Mine and your master, with his own hand gave me;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.299">Bade me enjoy it, with the place and honours,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.300">During my life; and, to confirm his goodness,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.301">Tied it by letters-patents: now, who'll take it?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech70"><b>SURREY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.302">The king, that gave it.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech71"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.303">It must be himself, then.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech72"><b>SURREY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.304">Thou art a proud traitor, priest.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech73"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.305">Proud lord, thou liest:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.306">Within these forty hours Surrey durst better</a><br>
<a name="3.2.307">Have burnt that tongue than said so.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech74"><b>SURREY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.308">Thy ambition,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.309">Thou scarlet sin, robb'd this bewailing land</a><br>
<a name="3.2.310">Of noble Buckingham, my father-in-law:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.311">The heads of all thy brother cardinals,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.312">With thee and all thy best parts bound together,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.313">Weigh'd not a hair of his. Plague of your policy!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.314">You sent me deputy for Ireland;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.315">Far from his succor, from the king, from all</a><br>
<a name="3.2.316">That might have mercy on the fault thou gavest him;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.317">Whilst your great goodness, out of holy pity,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.318">Absolved him with an axe.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech75"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.319">This, and all else</a><br>
<a name="3.2.320">This talking lord can lay upon my credit,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.321">I answer is most false. The duke by law</a><br>
<a name="3.2.322">Found his deserts: how innocent I was</a><br>
<a name="3.2.323">From any private malice in his end,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.324">His noble jury and foul cause can witness.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.325">If I loved many words, lord, I should tell you</a><br>
<a name="3.2.326">You have as little honesty as honour,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.327">That in the way of loyalty and truth</a><br>
<a name="3.2.328">Toward the king, my ever royal master,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.329">Dare mate a sounder man than Surrey can be,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.330">And all that love his follies.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech76"><b>SURREY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.331">By my soul,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.332">Your long coat, priest, protects you; thou</a><br>
<a name="3.2.333">shouldst feel</a><br>
<a name="3.2.334">My sword i' the life-blood of thee else. My lords,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.335">Can ye endure to hear this arrogance?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.336">And from this fellow? if we live thus tamely,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.337">To be thus jaded by a piece of scarlet,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.338">Farewell nobility; let his grace go forward,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.339">And dare us with his cap like larks.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech77"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.340">All goodness</a><br>
<a name="3.2.341">Is poison to thy stomach.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech78"><b>SURREY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.342">Yes, that goodness</a><br>
<a name="3.2.343">Of gleaning all the land's wealth into one,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.344">Into your own hands, cardinal, by extortion;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.345">The goodness of your intercepted packets</a><br>
<a name="3.2.346">You writ to the pope against the king: your goodness,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.347">Since you provoke me, shall be most notorious.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.348">My Lord of Norfolk, as you are truly noble,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.349">As you respect the common good, the state</a><br>
<a name="3.2.350">Of our despised nobility, our issues,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.351">Who, if he live, will scarce be gentlemen,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.352">Produce the grand sum of his sins, the articles</a><br>
<a name="3.2.353">Collected from his life. I'll startle you</a><br>
<a name="3.2.354">Worse than the scaring bell, when the brown wench</a><br>
<a name="3.2.355">Lay kissing in your arms, lord cardinal.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech79"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.356">How much, methinks, I could despise this man,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.357">But that I am bound in charity against it!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech80"><b>NORFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.358">Those articles, my lord, are in the king's hand:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.359">But, thus much, they are foul ones.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech81"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.360">So much fairer</a><br>
<a name="3.2.361">And spotless shall mine innocence arise,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.362">When the king knows my truth.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech82"><b>SURREY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.363">This cannot save you:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.364">I thank my memory, I yet remember</a><br>
<a name="3.2.365">Some of these articles; and out they shall.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.366">Now, if you can blush and cry 'guilty,' cardinal,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.367">You'll show a little honesty.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech83"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.368">Speak on, sir;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.369">I dare your worst objections: if I blush,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.370">It is to see a nobleman want manners.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech84"><b>SURREY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.371">I had rather want those than my head. Have at you!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.372">First, that, without the king's assent or knowledge,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.373">You wrought to be a legate; by which power</a><br>
<a name="3.2.374">You maim'd the jurisdiction of all bishops.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech85"><b>NORFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.375">Then, that in all you writ to Rome, or else</a><br>
<a name="3.2.376">To foreign princes, 'Ego et Rex meus'</a><br>
<a name="3.2.377">Was still inscribed; in which you brought the king</a><br>
<a name="3.2.378">To be your servant.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech86"><b>SUFFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.379">Then that, without the knowledge</a><br>
<a name="3.2.380">Either of king or council, when you went</a><br>
<a name="3.2.381">Ambassador to the emperor, you made bold</a><br>
<a name="3.2.382">To carry into Flanders the great seal.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech87"><b>SURREY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.383">Item, you sent a large commission</a><br>
<a name="3.2.384">To Gregory de Cassado, to conclude,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.385">Without the king's will or the state's allowance,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.386">A league between his highness and Ferrara.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech88"><b>SUFFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.387">That, out of mere ambition, you have caused</a><br>
<a name="3.2.388">Your holy hat to be stamp'd on the king's coin.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech89"><b>SURREY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.389">Then that you have sent innumerable substance--</a><br>
<a name="3.2.390">By what means got, I leave to your own conscience--</a><br>
<a name="3.2.391">To furnish Rome, and to prepare the ways</a><br>
<a name="3.2.392">You have for dignities; to the mere undoing</a><br>
<a name="3.2.393">Of all the kingdom. Many more there are;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.394">Which, since they are of you, and odious,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.395">I will not taint my mouth with.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech90"><b>Chamberlain</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.396">O my lord,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.397">Press not a falling man too far! 'tis virtue:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.398">His faults lie open to the laws; let them,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.399">Not you, correct him. My heart weeps to see him</a><br>
<a name="3.2.400">So little of his great self.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech91"><b>SURREY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.401">I forgive him.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech92"><b>SUFFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.402">Lord cardinal, the king's further pleasure is,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.403">Because all those things you have done of late,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.404">By your power legatine, within this kingdom,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.405">Fall into the compass of a praemunire,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.406">That therefore such a writ be sued against you;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.407">To forfeit all your goods, lands, tenements,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.408">Chattels, and whatsoever, and to be</a><br>
<a name="3.2.409">Out of the king's protection. This is my charge.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech93"><b>NORFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.410">And so we'll leave you to your meditations</a><br>
<a name="3.2.411">How to live better. For your stubborn answer</a><br>
<a name="3.2.412">About the giving back the great seal to us,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.413">The king shall know it, and, no doubt, shall thank you.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.414">So fare you well, my little good lord cardinal.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt all but CARDINAL WOLSEY</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech94"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.415">So farewell to the little good you bear me.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.416">Farewell! a long farewell, to all my greatness!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.417">This is the state of man: to-day he puts forth</a><br>
<a name="3.2.418">The tender leaves of hopes; to-morrow blossoms,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.419">And bears his blushing honours thick upon him;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.420">The third day comes a frost, a killing frost,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.421">And, when he thinks, good easy man, full surely</a><br>
<a name="3.2.422">His greatness is a-ripening, nips his root,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.423">And then he falls, as I do. I have ventured,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.424">Like little wanton boys that swim on bladders,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.425">This many summers in a sea of glory,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.426">But far beyond my depth: my high-blown pride</a><br>
<a name="3.2.427">At length broke under me and now has left me,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.428">Weary and old with service, to the mercy</a><br>
<a name="3.2.429">Of a rude stream, that must for ever hide me.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.430">Vain pomp and glory of this world, I hate ye:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.431">I feel my heart new open'd. O, how wretched</a><br>
<a name="3.2.432">Is that poor man that hangs on princes' favours!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.433">There is, betwixt that smile we would aspire to,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.434">That sweet aspect of princes, and their ruin,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.435">More pangs and fears than wars or women have:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.436">And when he falls, he falls like Lucifer,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.437">Never to hope again.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter CROMWELL, and stands amazed</i></p>
<a name="3.2.438">Why, how now, Cromwell!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech95"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.439">I have no power to speak, sir.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech96"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.440">What, amazed</a><br>
<a name="3.2.441">At my misfortunes? can thy spirit wonder</a><br>
<a name="3.2.442">A great man should decline? Nay, an you weep,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.443">I am fall'n indeed.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech97"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.444">How does your grace?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech98"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.445">Why, well;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.446">Never so truly happy, my good Cromwell.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.447">I know myself now; and I feel within me</a><br>
<a name="3.2.448">A peace above all earthly dignities,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.449">A still and quiet conscience. The king has cured me,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.450">I humbly thank his grace; and from these shoulders,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.451">These ruin'd pillars, out of pity, taken</a><br>
<a name="3.2.452">A load would sink a navy, too much honour:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.453">O, 'tis a burthen, Cromwell, 'tis a burthen</a><br>
<a name="3.2.454">Too heavy for a man that hopes for heaven!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech99"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.455">I am glad your grace has made that right use of it.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech100"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.456">I hope I have: I am able now, methinks,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.457">Out of a fortitude of soul I feel,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.458">To endure more miseries and greater far</a><br>
<a name="3.2.459">Than my weak-hearted enemies dare offer.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.460">What news abroad?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech101"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.461">                  The heaviest and the worst</a><br>
<a name="3.2.462">Is your displeasure with the king.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech102"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.463">God bless him!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech103"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.464">The next is, that Sir Thomas More is chosen</a><br>
<a name="3.2.465">Lord chancellor in your place.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech104"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.466">That's somewhat sudden:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.467">But he's a learned man. May he continue</a><br>
<a name="3.2.468">Long in his highness' favour, and do justice</a><br>
<a name="3.2.469">For truth's sake and his conscience; that his bones,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.470">When he has run his course and sleeps in blessings,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.471">May have a tomb of orphans' tears wept on em! What more?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech105"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.472">That Cranmer is return'd with welcome,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.473">Install'd lord archbishop of Canterbury.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech106"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.474">That's news indeed.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech107"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.475">Last, that the Lady Anne,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.476">Whom the king hath in secrecy long married,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.477">This day was view'd in open as his queen,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.478">Going to chapel; and the voice is now</a><br>
<a name="3.2.479">Only about her coronation.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech108"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.480">There was the weight that pull'd me down. O Cromwell,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.481">The king has gone beyond me: all my glories</a><br>
<a name="3.2.482">In that one woman I have lost for ever:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.483">No sun shall ever usher forth mine honours,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.484">Or gild again the noble troops that waited</a><br>
<a name="3.2.485">Upon my smiles. Go, get thee from me, Cromwell;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.486">I am a poor fall'n man, unworthy now</a><br>
<a name="3.2.487">To be thy lord and master: seek the king;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.488">That sun, I pray, may never set! I have told him</a><br>
<a name="3.2.489">What and how true thou art: he will advance thee;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.490">Some little memory of me will stir him--</a><br>
<a name="3.2.491">I know his noble nature--not to let</a><br>
<a name="3.2.492">Thy hopeful service perish too: good Cromwell,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.493">Neglect him not; make use now, and provide</a><br>
<a name="3.2.494">For thine own future safety.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech109"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.495">O my lord,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.496">Must I, then, leave you? must I needs forego</a><br>
<a name="3.2.497">So good, so noble and so true a master?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.498">Bear witness, all that have not hearts of iron,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.499">With what a sorrow Cromwell leaves his lord.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.500">The king shall have my service: but my prayers</a><br>
<a name="3.2.501">For ever and for ever shall be yours.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech110"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.502">Cromwell, I did not think to shed a tear</a><br>
<a name="3.2.503">In all my miseries; but thou hast forced me,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.504">Out of thy honest truth, to play the woman.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.505">Let's dry our eyes: and thus far hear me, Cromwell;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.506">And, when I am forgotten, as I shall be,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.507">And sleep in dull cold marble, where no mention</a><br>
<a name="3.2.508">Of me more must be heard of, say, I taught thee,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.509">Say, Wolsey, that once trod the ways of glory,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.510">And sounded all the depths and shoals of honour,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.511">Found thee a way, out of his wreck, to rise in;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.512">A sure and safe one, though thy master miss'd it.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.513">Mark but my fall, and that that ruin'd me.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.514">Cromwell, I charge thee, fling away ambition:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.515">By that sin fell the angels; how can man, then,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.516">The image of his Maker, hope to win by it?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.517">Love thyself last: cherish those hearts that hate thee;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.518">Corruption wins not more than honesty.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.519">Still in thy right hand carry gentle peace,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.520">To silence envious tongues. Be just, and fear not:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.521">Let all the ends thou aim'st at be thy country's,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.522">Thy God's, and truth's; then if thou fall'st,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.523">O Cromwell,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.524">Thou fall'st a blessed martyr! Serve the king;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.525">And,--prithee, lead me in:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.526">There take an inventory of all I have,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.527">To the last penny; 'tis the king's: my robe,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.528">And my integrity to heaven, is all</a><br>
<a name="3.2.529">I dare now call mine own. O Cromwell, Cromwell!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.530">Had I but served my God with half the zeal</a><br>
<a name="3.2.531">I served my king, he would not in mine age</a><br>
<a name="3.2.532">Have left me naked to mine enemies.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech111"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.533">Good sir, have patience.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech112"><b>CARDINAL WOLSEY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.534">So I have. Farewell</a><br>
<a name="3.2.535">The hopes of court! my hopes in heaven do dwell.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote><p>
</p><h3>ACT IV</h3>
<h3>SCENE I. A street in Westminster.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter two Gentlemen, meeting one another</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.1">You're well met once again.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.2">So are you.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.3">You come to take your stand here, and behold</a><br>
<a name="4.1.4">The Lady Anne pass from her coronation?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.5">'Tis all my business. At our last encounter,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.6">The Duke of Buckingham came from his trial.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.7">'Tis very true: but that time offer'd sorrow;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.8">This, general joy.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.9">'Tis well: the citizens,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.10">I am sure, have shown at full their royal minds--</a><br>
<a name="4.1.11">As, let 'em have their rights, they are ever forward--</a><br>
<a name="4.1.12">In celebration of this day with shows,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.13">Pageants and sights of honour.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.14">Never greater,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.15">Nor, I'll assure you, better taken, sir.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.16">May I be bold to ask at what that contains,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.17">That paper in your hand?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.18">Yes; 'tis the list</a><br>
<a name="4.1.19">Of those that claim their offices this day</a><br>
<a name="4.1.20">By custom of the coronation.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.21">The Duke of Suffolk is the first, and claims</a><br>
<a name="4.1.22">To be high-steward; next, the Duke of Norfolk,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.23">He to be earl marshal: you may read the rest.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.24">I thank you, sir: had I not known those customs,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.25">I should have been beholding to your paper.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.26">But, I beseech you, what's become of Katharine,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.27">The princess dowager? how goes her business?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.28">That I can tell you too. The Archbishop</a><br>
<a name="4.1.29">Of Canterbury, accompanied with other</a><br>
<a name="4.1.30">Learned and reverend fathers of his order,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.31">Held a late court at Dunstable, six miles off</a><br>
<a name="4.1.32">From Ampthill where the princess lay; to which</a><br>
<a name="4.1.33">She was often cited by them, but appear'd not:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.34">And, to be short, for not appearance and</a><br>
<a name="4.1.35">The king's late scruple, by the main assent</a><br>
<a name="4.1.36">Of all these learned men she was divorced,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.37">And the late marriage made of none effect</a><br>
<a name="4.1.38">Since which she was removed to Kimbolton,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.39">Where she remains now sick.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.40">Alas, good lady!</a><br>
<p><i>Trumpets</i></p>
<a name="4.1.41">The trumpets sound: stand close, the queen is coming.</a><br>
<p><i>Hautboys</i></p>
<p><i>THE ORDER OF THE CORONATION</i></p>
<a name="4.1.42">1. A lively flourish of Trumpets.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.43">2. Then, two Judges.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.44">3. Lord Chancellor, with the purse and mace</a><br>
<a name="4.1.45">before him.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.46">4. Choristers, singing.</a><br>
<p><i>Music</i></p>
<a name="4.1.47">5. Mayor of London, bearing the mace. Then</a><br>
<a name="4.1.48">Garter, in his coat of arms, and on his</a><br>
<a name="4.1.49">head a gilt copper crown.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.50">6. Marquess Dorset, bearing a sceptre of gold,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.51">on his head a demi-coronal of gold. With</a><br>
<a name="4.1.52">him, SURREY, bearing the rod of silver with</a><br>
<a name="4.1.53">the dove, crowned with an earl's coronet.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.54">Collars of SS.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.55">7. SUFFOLK, in his robe of estate, his coronet</a><br>
<a name="4.1.56">on his head, bearing a long white wand, as</a><br>
<a name="4.1.57">high-steward. With him, NORFOLK, with the</a><br>
<a name="4.1.58">rod of marshalship, a coronet on his head.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.59">Collars of SS.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.60">8. A canopy borne by four of the Cinque-ports;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.61">under it, QUEEN ANNE in her robe; in her hair</a><br>
<a name="4.1.62">richly adorned with pearl, crowned. On each</a><br>
<a name="4.1.63">side her, the Bishops of London and</a><br>
<a name="4.1.64">Winchester.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.65">9. The old Duchess of Norfolk, in a coronal of</a><br>
<a name="4.1.66">gold, wrought with flowers, bearing QUEEN</a><br>
<a name="4.1.67">ANNE's train.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.68">10. Certain Ladies or Countesses, with plain</a><br>
<a name="4.1.69">circlets of gold without flowers.</a><br>
<p><i>They pass over the stage in order and state</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.70">A royal train, believe me. These I know:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.71">Who's that that bears the sceptre?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.72">Marquess Dorset:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.73">And that the Earl of Surrey, with the rod.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.74">A bold brave gentleman. That should be</a><br>
<a name="4.1.75">The Duke of Suffolk?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.76">'Tis the same: high-steward.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.77">And that my Lord of Norfolk?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.78">Yes;</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech19"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.79">Heaven bless thee!</a><br>
<p><i>Looking on QUEEN ANNE</i></p>
<a name="4.1.80">Thou hast the sweetest face I ever look'd on.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.81">Sir, as I have a soul, she is an angel;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.82">Our king has all the Indies in his arms,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.83">And more and richer, when he strains that lady:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.84">I cannot blame his conscience.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech20"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.85">They that bear</a><br>
<a name="4.1.86">The cloth of honour over her, are four barons</a><br>
<a name="4.1.87">Of the Cinque-ports.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech21"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.88">Those men are happy; and so are all are near her.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.89">I take it, she that carries up the train</a><br>
<a name="4.1.90">Is that old noble lady, Duchess of Norfolk.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech22"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.91">It is; and all the rest are countesses.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech23"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.92">Their coronets say so. These are stars indeed;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.93">And sometimes falling ones.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech24"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.94">No more of that.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit procession, and then a great flourish of trumpets</i></p>
<p><i>Enter a third Gentleman</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech25"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.95">God save you, sir! where have you been broiling?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech26"><b>Third Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.96">Among the crowd i' the Abbey; where a finger</a><br>
<a name="4.1.97">Could not be wedged in more: I am stifled</a><br>
<a name="4.1.98">With the mere rankness of their joy.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech27"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.99">You saw</a><br>
<a name="4.1.100">The ceremony?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech28"><b>Third Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.101">                  That I did.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech29"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.102">How was it?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech30"><b>Third Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.103">Well worth the seeing.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech31"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.104">Good sir, speak it to us.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech32"><b>Third Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.105">As well as I am able. The rich stream</a><br>
<a name="4.1.106">Of lords and ladies, having brought the queen</a><br>
<a name="4.1.107">To a prepared place in the choir, fell off</a><br>
<a name="4.1.108">A distance from her; while her grace sat down</a><br>
<a name="4.1.109">To rest awhile, some half an hour or so,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.110">In a rich chair of state, opposing freely</a><br>
<a name="4.1.111">The beauty of her person to the people.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.112">Believe me, sir, she is the goodliest woman</a><br>
<a name="4.1.113">That ever lay by man: which when the people</a><br>
<a name="4.1.114">Had the full view of, such a noise arose</a><br>
<a name="4.1.115">As the shrouds make at sea in a stiff tempest,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.116">As loud, and to as many tunes: hats, cloaks--</a><br>
<a name="4.1.117">Doublets, I think,--flew up; and had their faces</a><br>
<a name="4.1.118">Been loose, this day they had been lost. Such joy</a><br>
<a name="4.1.119">I never saw before. Great-bellied women,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.120">That had not half a week to go, like rams</a><br>
<a name="4.1.121">In the old time of war, would shake the press,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.122">And make 'em reel before 'em. No man living</a><br>
<a name="4.1.123">Could say 'This is my wife' there; all were woven</a><br>
<a name="4.1.124">So strangely in one piece.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech33"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.125">But, what follow'd?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech34"><b>Third Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.126">At length her grace rose, and with modest paces</a><br>
<a name="4.1.127">Came to the altar; where she kneel'd, and saint-like</a><br>
<a name="4.1.128">Cast her fair eyes to heaven and pray'd devoutly.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.129">Then rose again and bow'd her to the people:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.130">When by the Archbishop of Canterbury</a><br>
<a name="4.1.131">She had all the royal makings of a queen;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.132">As holy oil, Edward Confessor's crown,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.133">The rod, and bird of peace, and all such emblems</a><br>
<a name="4.1.134">Laid nobly on her: which perform'd, the choir,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.135">With all the choicest music of the kingdom,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.136">Together sung 'Te Deum.' So she parted,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.137">And with the same full state paced back again</a><br>
<a name="4.1.138">To York-place, where the feast is held.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech35"><b>First Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.139">Sir,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.140">You must no more call it York-place, that's past;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.141">For, since the cardinal fell, that title's lost:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.142">'Tis now the king's, and call'd Whitehall.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech36"><b>Third Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.143">I know it;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.144">But 'tis so lately alter'd, that the old name</a><br>
<a name="4.1.145">Is fresh about me.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech37"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.146">                  What two reverend bishops</a><br>
<a name="4.1.147">Were those that went on each side of the queen?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech38"><b>Third Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.148">Stokesly and Gardiner; the one of Winchester,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.149">Newly preferr'd from the king's secretary,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.150">The other, London.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech39"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.151">                  He of Winchester</a><br>
<a name="4.1.152">Is held no great good lover of the archbishop's,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.153">The virtuous Cranmer.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech40"><b>Third Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.154">All the land knows that:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.155">However, yet there is no great breach; when it comes,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.156">Cranmer will find a friend will not shrink from him.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech41"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.157">Who may that be, I pray you?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech42"><b>Third Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.158">Thomas Cromwell;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.159">A man in much esteem with the king, and truly</a><br>
<a name="4.1.160">A worthy friend. The king has made him master</a><br>
<a name="4.1.161">O' the jewel house,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.162">And one, already, of the privy council.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech43"><b>Second Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.163">He will deserve more.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech44"><b>Third Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.164">Yes, without all doubt.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.165">Come, gentlemen, ye shall go my way, which</a><br>
<a name="4.1.166">Is to the court, and there ye shall be my guests:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.167">Something I can command. As I walk thither,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.168">I'll tell ye more.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech45"><b>Both</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.169">                  You may command us, sir.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE II. Kimbolton.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter KATHARINE, Dowager, sick; led between GRIFFITH, her gentleman usher, and PATIENCE, her woman</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>GRIFFITH</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.1">How does your grace?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.2">O Griffith, sick to death!</a><br>
<a name="4.2.3">My legs, like loaden branches, bow to the earth,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.4">Willing to leave their burthen. Reach a chair:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.5">So; now, methinks, I feel a little ease.</a><br>
<a name="4.2.6">Didst thou not tell me, Griffith, as thou led'st me,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.7">That the great child of honour, Cardinal Wolsey, Was dead?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>GRIFFITH</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.8">        Yes, madam; but I think your grace,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.9">Out of the pain you suffer'd, gave no ear to't.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.10">Prithee, good Griffith, tell me how he died:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.11">If well, he stepp'd before me, happily</a><br>
<a name="4.2.12">For my example.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>GRIFFITH</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.13">                  Well, the voice goes, madam:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.14">For after the stout Earl Northumberland</a><br>
<a name="4.2.15">Arrested him at York, and brought him forward,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.16">As a man sorely tainted, to his answer,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.17">He fell sick suddenly, and grew so ill</a><br>
<a name="4.2.18">He could not sit his mule.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.19">Alas, poor man!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>GRIFFITH</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.20">At last, with easy roads, he came to Leicester,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.21">Lodged in the abbey; where the reverend abbot,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.22">With all his covent, honourably received him;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.23">To whom he gave these words, 'O, father abbot,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.24">An old man, broken with the storms of state,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.25">Is come to lay his weary bones among ye;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.26">Give him a little earth for charity!'</a><br>
<a name="4.2.27">So went to bed; where eagerly his sickness</a><br>
<a name="4.2.28">Pursued him still: and, three nights after this,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.29">About the hour of eight, which he himself</a><br>
<a name="4.2.30">Foretold should be his last, full of repentance,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.31">Continual meditations, tears, and sorrows,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.32">He gave his honours to the world again,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.33">His blessed part to heaven, and slept in peace.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.34">So may he rest; his faults lie gently on him!</a><br>
<a name="4.2.35">Yet thus far, Griffith, give me leave to speak him,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.36">And yet with charity. He was a man</a><br>
<a name="4.2.37">Of an unbounded stomach, ever ranking</a><br>
<a name="4.2.38">Himself with princes; one that, by suggestion,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.39">Tied all the kingdom: simony was fair-play;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.40">His own opinion was his law: i' the presence</a><br>
<a name="4.2.41">He would say untruths; and be ever double</a><br>
<a name="4.2.42">Both in his words and meaning: he was never,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.43">But where he meant to ruin, pitiful:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.44">His promises were, as he then was, mighty;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.45">But his performance, as he is now, nothing:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.46">Of his own body he was ill, and gave</a><br>
<a name="4.2.47">The clergy in example.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>GRIFFITH</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.48">Noble madam,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.49">Men's evil manners live in brass; their virtues</a><br>
<a name="4.2.50">We write in water. May it please your highness</a><br>
<a name="4.2.51">To hear me speak his good now?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.52">Yes, good Griffith;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.53">I were malicious else.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>GRIFFITH</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.54">This cardinal,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.55">Though from an humble stock, undoubtedly</a><br>
<a name="4.2.56">Was fashion'd to much honour from his cradle.</a><br>
<a name="4.2.57">He was a scholar, and a ripe and good one;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.58">Exceeding wise, fair-spoken, and persuading:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.59">Lofty and sour to them that loved him not;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.60">But to those men that sought him sweet as summer.</a><br>
<a name="4.2.61">And though he were unsatisfied in getting,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.62">Which was a sin, yet in bestowing, madam,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.63">He was most princely: ever witness for him</a><br>
<a name="4.2.64">Those twins Of learning that he raised in you,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.65">Ipswich and Oxford! one of which fell with him,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.66">Unwilling to outlive the good that did it;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.67">The other, though unfinish'd, yet so famous,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.68">So excellent in art, and still so rising,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.69">That Christendom shall ever speak his virtue.</a><br>
<a name="4.2.70">His overthrow heap'd happiness upon him;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.71">For then, and not till then, he felt himself,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.72">And found the blessedness of being little:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.73">And, to add greater honours to his age</a><br>
<a name="4.2.74">Than man could give him, he died fearing God.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.75">After my death I wish no other herald,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.76">No other speaker of my living actions,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.77">To keep mine honour from corruption,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.78">But such an honest chronicler as Griffith.</a><br>
<a name="4.2.79">Whom I most hated living, thou hast made me,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.80">With thy religious truth and modesty,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.81">Now in his ashes honour: peace be with him!</a><br>
<a name="4.2.82">Patience, be near me still; and set me lower:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.83">I have not long to trouble thee. Good Griffith,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.84">Cause the musicians play me that sad note</a><br>
<a name="4.2.85">I named my knell, whilst I sit meditating</a><br>
<a name="4.2.86">On that celestial harmony I go to.</a><br>
<p><i>Sad and solemn music</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>GRIFFITH</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.87">She is asleep: good wench, let's sit down quiet,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.88">For fear we wake her: softly, gentle Patience.</a><br>
<p><i>The vision. Enter, solemnly tripping one after  another, six personages, clad in white robes,  wearing on their heads garlands of bays, and golden  vizards on their faces; branches of bays or palm in  their hands. They first congee unto her, then  dance; and, at certain changes, the first two hold  a spare garland over her head; at which the other  four make reverent curtsies; then the two that held  the garland deliver the same to the other next two,  who observe the same order in their changes, and  holding the garland over her head: which done,  they deliver the same garland to the last two, who  likewise observe the same order: at which, as it  were by inspiration, she makes in her sleep signs  of rejoicing, and holdeth up her hands to heaven:  and so in their dancing vanish, carrying the garland with them. The music continues</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.89">Spirits of peace, where are ye? are ye all gone,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.90">And leave me here in wretchedness behind ye?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>GRIFFITH</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.91">Madam, we are here.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.92">It is not you I call for:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.93">Saw ye none enter since I slept?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>GRIFFITH</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.94">None, madam.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.95">No? Saw you not, even now, a blessed troop</a><br>
<a name="4.2.96">Invite me to a banquet; whose bright faces</a><br>
<a name="4.2.97">Cast thousand beams upon me, like the sun?</a><br>
<a name="4.2.98">They promised me eternal happiness;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.99">And brought me garlands, Griffith, which I feel</a><br>
<a name="4.2.100">I am not worthy yet to wear: I shall, assuredly.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech19"><b>GRIFFITH</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.101">I am most joyful, madam, such good dreams</a><br>
<a name="4.2.102">Possess your fancy.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech20"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.103">Bid the music leave,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.104">They are harsh and heavy to me.</a><br>
<p><i>Music ceases</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech21"><b>PATIENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.105">Do you note</a><br>
<a name="4.2.106">How much her grace is alter'd on the sudden?</a><br>
<a name="4.2.107">How long her face is drawn? how pale she looks,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.108">And of an earthy cold? Mark her eyes!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech22"><b>GRIFFITH</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.109">She is going, wench: pray, pray.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech23"><b>PATIENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.110">Heaven comfort her!</a><br>
<p><i>Enter a Messenger</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech24"><b>Messenger</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.111">An't like your grace,--</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech25"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.112">You are a saucy fellow:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.113">Deserve we no more reverence?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech26"><b>GRIFFITH</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.114">You are to blame,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.115">Knowing she will not lose her wonted greatness,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.116">To use so rude behavior; go to, kneel.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech27"><b>Messenger</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.117">I humbly do entreat your highness' pardon;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.118">My haste made me unmannerly. There is staying</a><br>
<a name="4.2.119">A gentleman, sent from the king, to see you.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech28"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.120">Admit him entrance, Griffith: but this fellow</a><br>
<a name="4.2.121">Let me ne'er see again.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt GRIFFITH and Messenger</i></p>
<p><i>Re-enter GRIFFITH, with CAPUCIUS</i></p>
<a name="4.2.122">If my sight fail not,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.123">You should be lord ambassador from the emperor,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.124">My royal nephew, and your name Capucius.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech29"><b>CAPUCIUS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.125">Madam, the same; your servant.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech30"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.126">O, my lord,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.127">The times and titles now are alter'd strangely</a><br>
<a name="4.2.128">With me since first you knew me. But, I pray you,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.129">What is your pleasure with me?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech31"><b>CAPUCIUS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.130">Noble lady,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.131">First mine own service to your grace; the next,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.132">The king's request that I would visit you;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.133">Who grieves much for your weakness, and by me</a><br>
<a name="4.2.134">Sends you his princely commendations,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.135">And heartily entreats you take good comfort.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech32"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.136">O my good lord, that comfort comes too late;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.137">'Tis like a pardon after execution:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.138">That gentle physic, given in time, had cured me;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.139">But now I am past an comforts here, but prayers.</a><br>
<a name="4.2.140">How does his highness?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech33"><b>CAPUCIUS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.141">Madam, in good health.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech34"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.142">So may he ever do! and ever flourish,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.143">When I shal l dwell with worms, and my poor name</a><br>
<a name="4.2.144">Banish'd the kingdom! Patience, is that letter,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.145">I caused you write, yet sent away?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech35"><b>PATIENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.146">No, madam.</a><br>
<p><i>Giving it to KATHARINE</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech36"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.147">Sir, I most humbly pray you to deliver</a><br>
<a name="4.2.148">This to my lord the king.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech37"><b>CAPUCIUS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.149">Most willing, madam.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech38"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.150">In which I have commended to his goodness</a><br>
<a name="4.2.151">The model of our chaste loves, his young daughter;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.152">The dews of heaven fall thick in blessings on her!</a><br>
<a name="4.2.153">Beseeching him to give her virtuous breeding--</a><br>
<a name="4.2.154">She is young, and of a noble modest nature,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.155">I hope she will deserve well,--and a little</a><br>
<a name="4.2.156">To love her for her mother's sake, that loved him,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.157">Heaven knows how dearly. My next poor petition</a><br>
<a name="4.2.158">Is, that his noble grace would have some pity</a><br>
<a name="4.2.159">Upon my wretched women, that so long</a><br>
<a name="4.2.160">Have follow'd both my fortunes faithfully:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.161">Of which there is not one, I dare avow,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.162">And now I should not lie, but will deserve</a><br>
<a name="4.2.163">For virtue and true beauty of the soul,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.164">For honesty and decent carriage,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.165">A right good husband, let him be a noble</a><br>
<a name="4.2.166">And, sure, those men are happy that shall have 'em.</a><br>
<a name="4.2.167">The last is, for my men; they are the poorest,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.168">But poverty could never draw 'em from me;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.169">That they may have their wages duly paid 'em,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.170">And something over to remember me by:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.171">If heaven had pleased to have given me longer life</a><br>
<a name="4.2.172">And able means, we had not parted thus.</a><br>
<a name="4.2.173">These are the whole contents: and, good my lord,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.174">By that you love the dearest in this world,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.175">As you wish Christian peace to souls departed,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.176">Stand these poor people's friend, and urge the king</a><br>
<a name="4.2.177">To do me this last right.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech39"><b>CAPUCIUS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.178">By heaven, I will,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.179">Or let me lose the fashion of a man!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech40"><b>KATHARINE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.180">I thank you, honest lord. Remember me</a><br>
<a name="4.2.181">In all humility unto his highness:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.182">Say his long trouble now is passing</a><br>
<a name="4.2.183">Out of this world; tell him, in death I bless'd him,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.184">For so I will. Mine eyes grow dim. Farewell,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.185">My lord. Griffith, farewell. Nay, Patience,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.186">You must not leave me yet: I must to bed;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.187">Call in more women. When I am dead, good wench,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.188">Let me be used with honour: strew me over</a><br>
<a name="4.2.189">With maiden flowers, that all the world may know</a><br>
<a name="4.2.190">I was a chaste wife to my grave: embalm me,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.191">Then lay me forth: although unqueen'd, yet like</a><br>
<a name="4.2.192">A queen, and daughter to a king, inter me.</a><br>
<a name="4.2.193">I can no more.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt, leading KATHARINE</i></p>
</blockquote><p>
</p><h3>ACT V</h3>
<h3>SCENE I. London. A gallery in the palace.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter GARDINER, Bishop of Winchester, a Page with a torch before him, met by LOVELL</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.1">It's one o'clock, boy, is't not?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>Boy</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.2">It hath struck.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.3">These should be hours for necessities,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.4">Not for delights; times to repair our nature</a><br>
<a name="5.1.5">With comforting repose, and not for us</a><br>
<a name="5.1.6">To waste these times. Good hour of night, Sir Thomas!</a><br>
<a name="5.1.7">Whither so late?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>LOVELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.8">                  Came you from the king, my lord</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.9">I did, Sir Thomas: and left him at primero</a><br>
<a name="5.1.10">With the Duke of Suffolk.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>LOVELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.11">I must to him too,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.12">Before he go to bed. I'll take my leave.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.13">Not yet, Sir Thomas Lovell. What's the matter?</a><br>
<a name="5.1.14">It seems you are in haste: an if there be</a><br>
<a name="5.1.15">No great offence belongs to't, give your friend</a><br>
<a name="5.1.16">Some touch of your late business: affairs, that walk,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.17">As they say spirits do, at midnight, have</a><br>
<a name="5.1.18">In them a wilder nature than the business</a><br>
<a name="5.1.19">That seeks dispatch by day.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>LOVELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.20">My lord, I love you;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.21">And durst commend a secret to your ear</a><br>
<a name="5.1.22">Much weightier than this work. The queen's in labour,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.23">They say, in great extremity; and fear'd</a><br>
<a name="5.1.24">She'll with the labour end.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.25">The fruit she goes with</a><br>
<a name="5.1.26">I pray for heartily, that it may find</a><br>
<a name="5.1.27">Good time, and live: but for the stock, Sir Thomas,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.28">I wish it grubb'd up now.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>LOVELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.29">Methinks I could</a><br>
<a name="5.1.30">Cry the amen; and yet my conscience says</a><br>
<a name="5.1.31">She's a good creature, and, sweet lady, does</a><br>
<a name="5.1.32">Deserve our better wishes.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.33">But, sir, sir,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.34">Hear me, Sir Thomas: you're a gentleman</a><br>
<a name="5.1.35">Of mine own way; I know you wise, religious;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.36">And, let me tell you, it will ne'er be well,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.37">'Twill not, Sir Thomas Lovell, take't of me,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.38">Till Cranmer, Cromwell, her two hands, and she,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.39">Sleep in their graves.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>LOVELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.40">Now, sir, you speak of two</a><br>
<a name="5.1.41">The most remark'd i' the kingdom. As for Cromwell,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.42">Beside that of the jewel house, is made master</a><br>
<a name="5.1.43">O' the rolls, and the king's secretary; further, sir,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.44">Stands in the gap and trade of moe preferments,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.45">With which the time will load him. The archbishop</a><br>
<a name="5.1.46">Is the king's hand and tongue; and who dare speak</a><br>
<a name="5.1.47">One syllable against him?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.48">Yes, yes, Sir Thomas,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.49">There are that dare; and I myself have ventured</a><br>
<a name="5.1.50">To speak my mind of him: and indeed this day,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.51">Sir, I may tell it you, I think I have</a><br>
<a name="5.1.52">Incensed the lords o' the council, that he is,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.53">For so I know he is, they know he is,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.54">A most arch heretic, a pestilence</a><br>
<a name="5.1.55">That does infect the land: with which they moved</a><br>
<a name="5.1.56">Have broken with the king; who hath so far</a><br>
<a name="5.1.57">Given ear to our complaint, of his great grace</a><br>
<a name="5.1.58">And princely care foreseeing those fell mischiefs</a><br>
<a name="5.1.59">Our reasons laid before him, hath commanded</a><br>
<a name="5.1.60">To-morrow morning to the council-board</a><br>
<a name="5.1.61">He be convented. He's a rank weed, Sir Thomas,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.62">And we must root him out. From your affairs</a><br>
<a name="5.1.63">I hinder you too long: good night, Sir Thomas.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>LOVELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.64">Many good nights, my lord: I rest your servant.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt GARDINER and Page</i></p>
<p><i>Enter KING HENRY VIII and SUFFOLK</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.65">Charles, I will play no more tonight;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.66">My mind's not on't; you are too hard for me.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>SUFFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.67">Sir, I did never win of you before.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.68">But little, Charles;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.69">Nor shall not, when my fancy's on my play.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.70">Now, Lovell, from the queen what is the news?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>LOVELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.71">I could not personally deliver to her</a><br>
<a name="5.1.72">What you commanded me, but by her woman</a><br>
<a name="5.1.73">I sent your message; who return'd her thanks</a><br>
<a name="5.1.74">In the great'st humbleness, and desired your highness</a><br>
<a name="5.1.75">Most heartily to pray for her.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech19"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.76">What say'st thou, ha?</a><br>
<a name="5.1.77">To pray for her? what, is she crying out?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech20"><b>LOVELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.78">So said her woman; and that her sufferance made</a><br>
<a name="5.1.79">Almost each pang a death.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech21"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.80">Alas, good lady!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech22"><b>SUFFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.81">God safely quit her of her burthen, and</a><br>
<a name="5.1.82">With gentle travail, to the gladding of</a><br>
<a name="5.1.83">Your highness with an heir!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech23"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.84">'Tis midnight, Charles;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.85">Prithee, to bed; and in thy prayers remember</a><br>
<a name="5.1.86">The estate of my poor queen. Leave me alone;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.87">For I must think of that which company</a><br>
<a name="5.1.88">Would not be friendly to.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech24"><b>SUFFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.89">I wish your highness</a><br>
<a name="5.1.90">A quiet night; and my good mistress will</a><br>
<a name="5.1.91">Remember in my prayers.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech25"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.92">Charles, good night.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit SUFFOLK</i></p>
<p><i>Enter DENNY</i></p>
<a name="5.1.93">Well, sir, what follows?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech26"><b>DENNY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.94">Sir, I have brought my lord the archbishop,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.95">As you commanded me.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech27"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.96">Ha! Canterbury?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech28"><b>DENNY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.97">Ay, my good lord.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech29"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.98">'Tis true: where is he, Denny?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech30"><b>DENNY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.99">He attends your highness' pleasure.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit DENNY</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech31"><b>LOVELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.100">[Aside]  This is about that which the bishop spake:</a><br>
<a name="5.1.101">I am happily come hither.</a><br>
<p><i>Re-enter DENNY, with CRANMER</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech32"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.102">Avoid the gallery.</a><br>
<p><i>LOVELL seems to stay</i></p>
<a name="5.1.103">Ha! I have said. Be gone. What!</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt LOVELL and DENNY</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech33"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.104">[Aside]</a><br>
<a name="5.1.105">I am fearful: wherefore frowns he thus?</a><br>
<a name="5.1.106">'Tis his aspect of terror. All's not well.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech34"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.107">How now, my lord! you desire to know</a><br>
<a name="5.1.108">Wherefore I sent for you.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech35"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.109">[Kneeling]              It is my duty</a><br>
<a name="5.1.110">To attend your highness' pleasure.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech36"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.111">Pray you, arise,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.112">My good and gracious Lord of Canterbury.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.113">Come, you and I must walk a turn together;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.114">I have news to tell you: come, come, give me your hand.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.115">Ah, my good lord, I grieve at what I speak,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.116">And am right sorry to repeat what follows</a><br>
<a name="5.1.117">I have, and most unwillingly, of late</a><br>
<a name="5.1.118">Heard many grievous, I do say, my lord,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.119">Grievous complaints of you; which, being consider'd,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.120">Have moved us and our council, that you shall</a><br>
<a name="5.1.121">This morning come before us; where, I know,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.122">You cannot with such freedom purge yourself,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.123">But that, till further trial in those charges</a><br>
<a name="5.1.124">Which will require your answer, you must take</a><br>
<a name="5.1.125">Your patience to you, and be well contented</a><br>
<a name="5.1.126">To make your house our Tower: you a brother of us,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.127">It fits we thus proceed, or else no witness</a><br>
<a name="5.1.128">Would come against you.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech37"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.129">[Kneeling]</a><br>
<a name="5.1.130">I humbly thank your highness;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.131">And am right glad to catch this good occasion</a><br>
<a name="5.1.132">Most throughly to be winnow'd, where my chaff</a><br>
<a name="5.1.133">And corn shall fly asunder: for, I know,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.134">There's none stands under more calumnious tongues</a><br>
<a name="5.1.135">Than I myself, poor man.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech38"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.136">Stand up, good Canterbury:</a><br>
<a name="5.1.137">Thy truth and thy integrity is rooted</a><br>
<a name="5.1.138">In us, thy friend: give me thy hand, stand up:</a><br>
<a name="5.1.139">Prithee, let's walk. Now, by my holidame.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.140">What manner of man are you? My lord, I look'd</a><br>
<a name="5.1.141">You would have given me your petition, that</a><br>
<a name="5.1.142">I should have ta'en some pains to bring together</a><br>
<a name="5.1.143">Yourself and your accusers; and to have heard you,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.144">Without indurance, further.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech39"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.145">Most dread liege,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.146">The good I stand on is my truth and honesty:</a><br>
<a name="5.1.147">If they shall fail, I, with mine enemies,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.148">Will triumph o'er my person; which I weigh not,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.149">Being of those virtues vacant. I fear nothing</a><br>
<a name="5.1.150">What can be said against me.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech40"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.151">Know you not</a><br>
<a name="5.1.152">How your state stands i' the world, with the whole world?</a><br>
<a name="5.1.153">Your enemies are many, and not small; their practises</a><br>
<a name="5.1.154">Must bear the same proportion; and not ever</a><br>
<a name="5.1.155">The justice and the truth o' the question carries</a><br>
<a name="5.1.156">The due o' the verdict with it: at what ease</a><br>
<a name="5.1.157">Might corrupt minds procure knaves as corrupt</a><br>
<a name="5.1.158">To swear against you? such things have been done.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.159">You are potently opposed; and with a malice</a><br>
<a name="5.1.160">Of as great size. Ween you of better luck,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.161">I mean, in perjured witness, than your master,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.162">Whose minister you are, whiles here he lived</a><br>
<a name="5.1.163">Upon this naughty earth? Go to, go to;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.164">You take a precipice for no leap of danger,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.165">And woo your own destruction.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech41"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.166">God and your majesty</a><br>
<a name="5.1.167">Protect mine innocence, or I fall into</a><br>
<a name="5.1.168">The trap is laid for me!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech42"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.169">Be of good cheer;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.170">They shall no more prevail than we give way to.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.171">Keep comfort to you; and this morning see</a><br>
<a name="5.1.172">You do appear before them: if they shall chance,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.173">In charging you with matters, to commit you,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.174">The best persuasions to the contrary</a><br>
<a name="5.1.175">Fail not to use, and with what vehemency</a><br>
<a name="5.1.176">The occasion shall instruct you: if entreaties</a><br>
<a name="5.1.177">Will render you no remedy, this ring</a><br>
<a name="5.1.178">Deliver them, and your appeal to us</a><br>
<a name="5.1.179">There make before them. Look, the good man weeps!</a><br>
<a name="5.1.180">He's honest, on mine honour. God's blest mother!</a><br>
<a name="5.1.181">I swear he is true--hearted; and a soul</a><br>
<a name="5.1.182">None better in my kingdom. Get you gone,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.183">And do as I have bid you.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit CRANMER</i></p>
<a name="5.1.184">He has strangled</a><br>
<a name="5.1.185">His language in his tears.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter Old Lady, LOVELL following</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech43"><b>Gentleman</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.186">[Within]                 Come back: what mean you?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech44"><b>Old Lady</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.187">I'll not come back; the tidings that I bring</a><br>
<a name="5.1.188">Will make my boldness manners. Now, good angels</a><br>
<a name="5.1.189">Fly o'er thy royal head, and shade thy person</a><br>
<a name="5.1.190">Under their blessed wings!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech45"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.191">Now, by thy looks</a><br>
<a name="5.1.192">I guess thy message. Is the queen deliver'd?</a><br>
<a name="5.1.193">Say, ay; and of a boy.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech46"><b>Old Lady</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.194">Ay, ay, my liege;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.195">And of a lovely boy: the God of heaven</a><br>
<a name="5.1.196">Both now and ever bless her! 'tis a girl,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.197">Promises boys hereafter. Sir, your queen</a><br>
<a name="5.1.198">Desires your visitation, and to be</a><br>
<a name="5.1.199">Acquainted with this stranger 'tis as like you</a><br>
<a name="5.1.200">As cherry is to cherry.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech47"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.201">Lovell!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech48"><b>LOVELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.202">Sir?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech49"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.203">Give her an hundred marks. I'll to the queen.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech50"><b>Old Lady</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.204">An hundred marks! By this light, I'll ha' more.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.205">An ordinary groom is for such payment.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.206">I will have more, or scold it out of him.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.207">Said I for this, the girl was like to him?</a><br>
<a name="5.1.208">I will have more, or else unsay't; and now,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.209">While it is hot, I'll put it to the issue.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE II. Before the council-chamber. Pursuivants, Pages,  &amp; c.</h3>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.1">attending.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter CRANMER</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.2">I hope I am not too late; and yet the gentleman,</a><br>
<a name="5.2.3">That was sent to me from the council, pray'd me</a><br>
<a name="5.2.4">To make great haste. All fast? what means this? Ho!</a><br>
<a name="5.2.5">Who waits there? Sure, you know me?</a><br>
<p><i>Enter Keeper</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>Keeper</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.6">Yes, my lord;</a><br>
<a name="5.2.7">But yet I cannot help you.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.8">Why?</a><br>
<p><i>Enter DOCTOR BUTTS</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>Keeper</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.9">Your grace must wait till you be call'd for.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.10">So.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>DOCTOR BUTTS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.11">[Aside]  This is a piece of malice. I am glad</a><br>
<a name="5.2.12">I came this way so happily: the king</a><br>
<a name="5.2.13">Shall understand it presently.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.14">[Aside]	'Tis Butts,</a><br>
<a name="5.2.15">The king's physician: as he pass'd along,</a><br>
<a name="5.2.16">How earnestly he cast his eyes upon me!</a><br>
<a name="5.2.17">Pray heaven, he sound not my disgrace! For certain,</a><br>
<a name="5.2.18">This is of purpose laid by some that hate me--</a><br>
<a name="5.2.19">God turn their hearts! I never sought their malice--</a><br>
<a name="5.2.20">To quench mine honour: they would shame to make me</a><br>
<a name="5.2.21">Wait else at door, a fellow-counsellor,</a><br>
<a name="5.2.22">'Mong boys, grooms, and lackeys. But their pleasures</a><br>
<a name="5.2.23">Must be fulfill'd, and I attend with patience.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter the KING HENRY VIII and DOCTOR BUTTS at a window above</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>DOCTOR BUTTS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.24">I'll show your grace the strangest sight--</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.25">What's that, Butts?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>DOCTOR BUTTS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.26">I think your highness saw this many a day.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.27">Body o' me, where is it?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>DOCTOR BUTTS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.28">There, my lord:</a><br>
<a name="5.2.29">The high promotion of his grace of Canterbury;</a><br>
<a name="5.2.30">Who holds his state at door, 'mongst pursuivants,</a><br>
<a name="5.2.31">Pages, and footboys.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.32">Ha! 'tis he, indeed:</a><br>
<a name="5.2.33">Is this the honour they do one another?</a><br>
<a name="5.2.34">'Tis well there's one above 'em yet. I had thought</a><br>
<a name="5.2.35">They had parted so much honesty among 'em</a><br>
<a name="5.2.36">At least, good manners, as not thus to suffer</a><br>
<a name="5.2.37">A man of his place, and so near our favour,</a><br>
<a name="5.2.38">To dance attendance on their lordships' pleasures,</a><br>
<a name="5.2.39">And at the door too, like a post with packets.</a><br>
<a name="5.2.40">By holy Mary, Butts, there's knavery:</a><br>
<a name="5.2.41">Let 'em alone, and draw the curtain close:</a><br>
<a name="5.2.42">We shall hear more anon.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE III. The Council-Chamber.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter Chancellor; places himself at the upper end  of the table on the left hand; a seat being left  void above him, as for CRANMER's seat. SUFFOLK,  NORFOLK, SURREY, Chamberlain, GARDINER, seat  themselves in order on each side. CROMWELL at lower end, as secretary. Keeper at the door</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>Chancellor</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.1">Speak to the business, master-secretary:</a><br>
<a name="5.3.2">Why are we met in council?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.3">Please your honours,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.4">The chief cause concerns his grace of Canterbury.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.5">Has he had knowledge of it?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.6">Yes.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>NORFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.7">Who waits there?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>Keeper</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.8">Without, my noble lords?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.9">Yes.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>Keeper</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.10">My lord archbishop;</a><br>
<a name="5.3.11">And has done half an hour, to know your pleasures.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>Chancellor</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.12">Let him come in.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>Keeper</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.13">                  Your grace may enter now.</a><br>
<p><i>CRANMER enters and approaches the council-table</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>Chancellor</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.14">My good lord archbishop, I'm very sorry</a><br>
<a name="5.3.15">To sit here at this present, and behold</a><br>
<a name="5.3.16">That chair stand empty: but we all are men,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.17">In our own natures frail, and capable</a><br>
<a name="5.3.18">Of our flesh; few are angels: out of which frailty</a><br>
<a name="5.3.19">And want of wisdom, you, that best should teach us,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.20">Have misdemean'd yourself, and not a little,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.21">Toward the king first, then his laws, in filling</a><br>
<a name="5.3.22">The whole realm, by your teaching and your chaplains,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.23">For so we are inform'd, with new opinions,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.24">Divers and dangerous; which are heresies,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.25">And, not reform'd, may prove pernicious.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.26">Which reformation must be sudden too,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.27">My noble lords; for those that tame wild horses</a><br>
<a name="5.3.28">Pace 'em not in their hands to make 'em gentle,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.29">But stop their mouths with stubborn bits, and spur 'em,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.30">Till they obey the manage. If we suffer,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.31">Out of our easiness and childish pity</a><br>
<a name="5.3.32">To one man's honour, this contagious sickness,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.33">Farewell all physic: and what follows then?</a><br>
<a name="5.3.34">Commotions, uproars, with a general taint</a><br>
<a name="5.3.35">Of the whole state: as, of late days, our neighbours,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.36">The upper Germany, can dearly witness,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.37">Yet freshly pitied in our memories.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.38">My good lords, hitherto, in all the progress</a><br>
<a name="5.3.39">Both of my life and office, I have labour'd,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.40">And with no little study, that my teaching</a><br>
<a name="5.3.41">And the strong course of my authority</a><br>
<a name="5.3.42">Might go one way, and safely; and the end</a><br>
<a name="5.3.43">Was ever, to do well: nor is there living,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.44">I speak it with a single heart, my lords,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.45">A man that more detests, more stirs against,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.46">Both in his private conscience and his place,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.47">Defacers of a public peace, than I do.</a><br>
<a name="5.3.48">Pray heaven, the king may never find a heart</a><br>
<a name="5.3.49">With less allegiance in it! Men that make</a><br>
<a name="5.3.50">Envy and crooked malice nourishment</a><br>
