<a name="5.3.51">Dare bite the best. I do beseech your lordships,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.52">That, in this case of justice, my accusers,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.53">Be what they will, may stand forth face to face,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.54">And freely urge against me.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>SUFFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.55">Nay, my lord,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.56">That cannot be: you are a counsellor,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.57">And, by that virtue, no man dare accuse you.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.58">My lord, because we have business of more moment,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.59">We will be short with you. 'Tis his highness' pleasure,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.60">And our consent, for better trial of you,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.61">From hence you be committed to the Tower;</a><br>
<a name="5.3.62">Where, being but a private man again,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.63">You shall know many dare accuse you boldly,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.64">More than, I fear, you are provided for.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.65">Ah, my good Lord of Winchester, I thank you;</a><br>
<a name="5.3.66">You are always my good friend; if your will pass,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.67">I shall both find your lordship judge and juror,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.68">You are so merciful: I see your end;</a><br>
<a name="5.3.69">'Tis my undoing: love and meekness, lord,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.70">Become a churchman better than ambition:</a><br>
<a name="5.3.71">Win straying souls with modesty again,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.72">Cast none away. That I shall clear myself,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.73">Lay all the weight ye can upon my patience,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.74">I make as little doubt, as you do conscience</a><br>
<a name="5.3.75">In doing daily wrongs. I could say more,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.76">But reverence to your calling makes me modest.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.77">My lord, my lord, you are a sectary,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.78">That's the plain truth: your painted gloss discovers,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.79">To men that understand you, words and weakness.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.80">My Lord of Winchester, you are a little,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.81">By your good favour, too sharp; men so noble,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.82">However faulty, yet should find respect</a><br>
<a name="5.3.83">For what they have been: 'tis a cruelty</a><br>
<a name="5.3.84">To load a falling man.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech19"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.85">Good master secretary,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.86">I cry your honour mercy; you may, worst</a><br>
<a name="5.3.87">Of all this table, say so.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech20"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.88">Why, my lord?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech21"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.89">Do not I know you for a favourer</a><br>
<a name="5.3.90">Of this new sect? ye are not sound.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech22"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.91">Not sound?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech23"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.92">Not sound, I say.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech24"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.93">                  Would you were half so honest!</a><br>
<a name="5.3.94">Men's prayers then would seek you, not their fears.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech25"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.95">I shall remember this bold language.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech26"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.96">Do.</a><br>
<a name="5.3.97">Remember your bold life too.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech27"><b>Chancellor</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.98">This is too much;</a><br>
<a name="5.3.99">Forbear, for shame, my lords.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech28"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.100">I have done.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech29"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.101">And I.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech30"><b>Chancellor</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.102">Then thus for you, my lord: it stands agreed,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.103">I take it, by all voices, that forthwith</a><br>
<a name="5.3.104">You be convey'd to the Tower a prisoner;</a><br>
<a name="5.3.105">There to remain till the king's further pleasure</a><br>
<a name="5.3.106">Be known unto us: are you all agreed, lords?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech31"><b>All</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.107">We are.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech32"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.108">      Is there no other way of mercy,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.109">But I must needs to the Tower, my lords?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech33"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.110">What other</a><br>
<a name="5.3.111">Would you expect? you are strangely troublesome.</a><br>
<a name="5.3.112">Let some o' the guard be ready there.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter Guard</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech34"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.113">For me?</a><br>
<a name="5.3.114">Must I go like a traitor thither?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech35"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.115">Receive him,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.116">And see him safe i' the Tower.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech36"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.117">Stay, good my lords,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.118">I have a little yet to say. Look there, my lords;</a><br>
<a name="5.3.119">By virtue of that ring, I take my cause</a><br>
<a name="5.3.120">Out of the gripes of cruel men, and give it</a><br>
<a name="5.3.121">To a most noble judge, the king my master.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech37"><b>Chamberlain</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.122">This is the king's ring.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech38"><b>SURREY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.123">'Tis no counterfeit.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech39"><b>SUFFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.124">'Tis the right ring, by heaven: I told ye all,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.125">When ye first put this dangerous stone a-rolling,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.126">'Twould fall upon ourselves.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech40"><b>NORFOLK</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.127">Do you think, my lords,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.128">The king will suffer but the little finger</a><br>
<a name="5.3.129">Of this man to be vex'd?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech41"><b>Chancellor</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.130">'Tis now too certain:</a><br>
<a name="5.3.131">How much more is his life in value with him?</a><br>
<a name="5.3.132">Would I were fairly out on't!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech42"><b>CROMWELL</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.133">My mind gave me,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.134">In seeking tales and informations</a><br>
<a name="5.3.135">Against this man, whose honesty the devil</a><br>
<a name="5.3.136">And his disciples only envy at,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.137">Ye blew the fire that burns ye: now have at ye!</a><br>
<p><i>Enter KING, frowning on them; takes his seat</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech43"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.138">Dread sovereign, how much are we bound to heaven</a><br>
<a name="5.3.139">In daily thanks, that gave us such a prince;</a><br>
<a name="5.3.140">Not only good and wise, but most religious:</a><br>
<a name="5.3.141">One that, in all obedience, makes the church</a><br>
<a name="5.3.142">The chief aim of his honour; and, to strengthen</a><br>
<a name="5.3.143">That holy duty, out of dear respect,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.144">His royal self in judgment comes to hear</a><br>
<a name="5.3.145">The cause betwixt her and this great offender.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech44"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.146">You were ever good at sudden commendations,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.147">Bishop of Winchester. But know, I come not</a><br>
<a name="5.3.148">To hear such flattery now, and in my presence;</a><br>
<a name="5.3.149">They are too thin and bare to hide offences.</a><br>
<a name="5.3.150">To me you cannot reach, you play the spaniel,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.151">And think with wagging of your tongue to win me;</a><br>
<a name="5.3.152">But, whatsoe'er thou takest me for, I'm sure</a><br>
<a name="5.3.153">Thou hast a cruel nature and a bloody.</a><br>
<p><i>To CRANMER</i></p>
<a name="5.3.154">Good man, sit down. Now let me see the proudest</a><br>
<a name="5.3.155">He, that dares most, but wag his finger at thee:</a><br>
<a name="5.3.156">By all that's holy, he had better starve</a><br>
<a name="5.3.157">Than but once think this place becomes thee not.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech45"><b>SURREY</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.158">May it please your grace,--</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech46"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.159">No, sir, it does not please me.</a><br>
<a name="5.3.160">I had thought I had had men of some understanding</a><br>
<a name="5.3.161">And wisdom of my council; but I find none.</a><br>
<a name="5.3.162">Was it discretion, lords, to let this man,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.163">This good man,--few of you deserve that title,--</a><br>
<a name="5.3.164">This honest man, wait like a lousy footboy</a><br>
<a name="5.3.165">At chamber--door? and one as great as you are?</a><br>
<a name="5.3.166">Why, what a shame was this! Did my commission</a><br>
<a name="5.3.167">Bid ye so far forget yourselves? I gave ye</a><br>
<a name="5.3.168">Power as he was a counsellor to try him,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.169">Not as a groom: there's some of ye, I see,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.170">More out of malice than integrity,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.171">Would try him to the utmost, had ye mean;</a><br>
<a name="5.3.172">Which ye shall never have while I live.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech47"><b>Chancellor</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.173">Thus far,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.174">My most dread sovereign, may it like your grace</a><br>
<a name="5.3.175">To let my tongue excuse all. What was purposed</a><br>
<a name="5.3.176">Concerning his imprisonment, was rather,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.177">If there be faith in men, meant for his trial,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.178">And fair purgation to the world, than malice,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.179">I'm sure, in me.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech48"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.180">Well, well, my lords, respect him;</a><br>
<a name="5.3.181">Take him, and use him well, he's worthy of it.</a><br>
<a name="5.3.182">I will say thus much for him, if a prince</a><br>
<a name="5.3.183">May be beholding to a subject, I</a><br>
<a name="5.3.184">Am, for his love and service, so to him.</a><br>
<a name="5.3.185">Make me no more ado, but all embrace him:</a><br>
<a name="5.3.186">Be friends, for shame, my lords! My Lord of</a><br>
<a name="5.3.187">Canterbury,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.188">I have a suit which you must not deny me;</a><br>
<a name="5.3.189">That is, a fair young maid that yet wants baptism,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.190">You must be godfather, and answer for her.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech49"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.191">The greatest monarch now alive may glory</a><br>
<a name="5.3.192">In such an honour: how may I deserve it</a><br>
<a name="5.3.193">That am a poor and humble subject to you?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech50"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.194">Come, come, my lord, you'ld spare your spoons: you</a><br>
<a name="5.3.195">shall have two noble partners with you; the old</a><br>
<a name="5.3.196">Duchess of Norfolk, and Lady Marquess Dorset: will</a><br>
<a name="5.3.197">these please you?</a><br>
<a name="5.3.198">Once more, my Lord of Winchester, I charge you,</a><br>
<a name="5.3.199">Embrace and love this man.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech51"><b>GARDINER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.200">With a true heart</a><br>
<a name="5.3.201">And brother-love I do it.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech52"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.202">And let heaven</a><br>
<a name="5.3.203">Witness, how dear I hold this confirmation.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech53"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.3.204">Good man, those joyful tears show thy true heart:</a><br>
<a name="5.3.205">The common voice, I see, is verified</a><br>
<a name="5.3.206">Of thee, which says thus, 'Do my Lord of Canterbury</a><br>
<a name="5.3.207">A shrewd turn, and he is your friend for ever.'</a><br>
<a name="5.3.208">Come, lords, we trifle time away; I long</a><br>
<a name="5.3.209">To have this young one made a Christian.</a><br>
<a name="5.3.210">As I have made ye one, lords, one remain;</a><br>
<a name="5.3.211">So I grow stronger, you more honour gain.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE IV. The palace yard.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Noise and tumult within. Enter Porter and his Man</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>Porter</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.1">You'll leave your noise anon, ye rascals: do you</a><br>
<a name="5.4.2">take the court for Paris-garden? ye rude slaves,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.3">leave your gaping.</a><br>
<p><i>Within</i></p>
<a name="5.4.4">Good master porter, I belong to the larder.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>Porter</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.5">Belong to the gallows, and be hanged, ye rogue! is</a><br>
<a name="5.4.6">this a place to roar in? Fetch me a dozen crab-tree</a><br>
<a name="5.4.7">staves, and strong ones: these are but switches to</a><br>
<a name="5.4.8">'em. I'll scratch your heads: you must be seeing</a><br>
<a name="5.4.9">christenings? do you look for ale and cakes here,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.10">you rude rascals?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>Man</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.11">Pray, sir, be patient: 'tis as much impossible--</a><br>
<a name="5.4.12">Unless we sweep 'em from the door with cannons--</a><br>
<a name="5.4.13">To scatter 'em, as 'tis to make 'em sleep</a><br>
<a name="5.4.14">On May-day morning; which will never be:</a><br>
<a name="5.4.15">We may as well push against Powle's, as stir em.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>Porter</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.16">How got they in, and be hang'd?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>Man</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.17">Alas, I know not; how gets the tide in?</a><br>
<a name="5.4.18">As much as one sound cudgel of four foot--</a><br>
<a name="5.4.19">You see the poor remainder--could distribute,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.20">I made no spare, sir.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>Porter</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.21">You did nothing, sir.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>Man</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.22">I am not Samson, nor Sir Guy, nor Colbrand,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.23">To mow 'em down before me: but if I spared any</a><br>
<a name="5.4.24">That had a head to hit, either young or old,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.25">He or she, cuckold or cuckold-maker,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.26">Let me ne'er hope to see a chine again</a><br>
<a name="5.4.27">And that I would not for a cow, God save her!</a><br>
<p><i>Within</i></p>
<a name="5.4.28">Do you hear, master porter?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>Porter</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.29">I shall be with you presently, good master puppy.</a><br>
<a name="5.4.30">Keep the door close, sirrah.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>Man</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.31">What would you have me do?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>Porter</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.32">What should you do, but knock 'em down by the</a><br>
<a name="5.4.33">dozens? Is this Moorfields to muster in? or have</a><br>
<a name="5.4.34">we some strange Indian with the great tool come to</a><br>
<a name="5.4.35">court, the women so besiege us? Bless me, what a</a><br>
<a name="5.4.36">fry of fornication is at door! On my Christian</a><br>
<a name="5.4.37">conscience, this one christening will beget a</a><br>
<a name="5.4.38">thousand; here will be father, godfather, and all together.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>Man</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.39">The spoons will be the bigger, sir. There is a</a><br>
<a name="5.4.40">fellow somewhat near the door, he should be a</a><br>
<a name="5.4.41">brazier by his face, for, o' my conscience, twenty</a><br>
<a name="5.4.42">of the dog-days now reign in's nose; all that stand</a><br>
<a name="5.4.43">about him are under the line, they need no other</a><br>
<a name="5.4.44">penance: that fire-drake did I hit three times on</a><br>
<a name="5.4.45">the head, and three times was his nose discharged</a><br>
<a name="5.4.46">against me; he stands there, like a mortar-piece, to</a><br>
<a name="5.4.47">blow us. There was a haberdasher's wife of small</a><br>
<a name="5.4.48">wit near him, that railed upon me till her pinked</a><br>
<a name="5.4.49">porringer fell off her head, for kindling such a</a><br>
<a name="5.4.50">combustion in the state. I missed the meteor once,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.51">and hit that woman; who cried out 'Clubs!' when I</a><br>
<a name="5.4.52">might see from far some forty truncheoners draw to</a><br>
<a name="5.4.53">her succor, which were the hope o' the Strand, where</a><br>
<a name="5.4.54">she was quartered. They fell on; I made good my</a><br>
<a name="5.4.55">place: at length they came to the broom-staff to</a><br>
<a name="5.4.56">me; I defied 'em still: when suddenly a file of</a><br>
<a name="5.4.57">boys behind 'em, loose shot, delivered such a shower</a><br>
<a name="5.4.58">of pebbles, that I was fain to draw mine honour in,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.59">and let 'em win the work: the devil was amongst</a><br>
<a name="5.4.60">'em, I think, surely.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>Porter</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.61">These are the youths that thunder at a playhouse,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.62">and fight for bitten apples; that no audience, but</a><br>
<a name="5.4.63">the tribulation of Tower-hill, or the limbs of</a><br>
<a name="5.4.64">Limehouse, their dear brothers, are able to endure.</a><br>
<a name="5.4.65">I have some of 'em in Limbo Patrum, and there they</a><br>
<a name="5.4.66">are like to dance these three days; besides the</a><br>
<a name="5.4.67">running banquet of two beadles that is to come.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter Chamberlain</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>Chamberlain</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.68">Mercy o' me, what a multitude are here!</a><br>
<a name="5.4.69">They grow still too; from all parts they are coming,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.70">As if we kept a fair here! Where are these porters,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.71">These lazy knaves? Ye have made a fine hand, fellows:</a><br>
<a name="5.4.72">There's a trim rabble let in: are all these</a><br>
<a name="5.4.73">Your faithful friends o' the suburbs? We shall have</a><br>
<a name="5.4.74">Great store of room, no doubt, left for the ladies,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.75">When they pass back from the christening.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>Porter</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.76">An't please</a><br>
<a name="5.4.77">your honour,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.78">We are but men; and what so many may do,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.79">Not being torn a-pieces, we have done:</a><br>
<a name="5.4.80">An army cannot rule 'em.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>Chamberlain</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.81">As I live,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.82">If the king blame me for't, I'll lay ye all</a><br>
<a name="5.4.83">By the heels, and suddenly; and on your heads</a><br>
<a name="5.4.84">Clap round fines for neglect: ye are lazy knaves;</a><br>
<a name="5.4.85">And here ye lie baiting of bombards, when</a><br>
<a name="5.4.86">Ye should do service. Hark! the trumpets sound;</a><br>
<a name="5.4.87">They're come already from the christening:</a><br>
<a name="5.4.88">Go, break among the press, and find a way out</a><br>
<a name="5.4.89">To let the troop pass fairly; or I'll find</a><br>
<a name="5.4.90">A Marshalsea shall hold ye play these two months.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>Porter</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.91">Make way there for the princess.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>Man</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.92">You great fellow,</a><br>
<a name="5.4.93">Stand close up, or I'll make your head ache.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>Porter</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.4.94">You i' the camlet, get up o' the rail;</a><br>
<a name="5.4.95">I'll peck you o'er the pales else.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE V. The palace.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter trumpets, sounding; then two Aldermen, Lord  Mayor, Garter, CRANMER, NORFOLK with his marshal's  staff, SUFFOLK, two Noblemen bearing great  standing-bowls for the christening-gifts; then  four Noblemen bearing a canopy, under which the  Duchess of Norfolk, godmother, bearing the child  richly habited in a mantle,  &amp; c., train borne by a  Lady; then follows the Marchioness Dorset, the  other godmother, and Ladies. The troop pass once about the stage, and Garter speaks</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>Garter</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.5.1">Heaven, from thy endless goodness, send prosperous</a><br>
<a name="5.5.2">life, long, and ever happy, to the high and mighty</a><br>
<a name="5.5.3">princess of England, Elizabeth!</a><br>
<p><i>Flourish. Enter KING HENRY VIII and Guard</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.5.4">[Kneeling] And to your royal grace, and the good queen,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.5">My noble partners, and myself, thus pray:</a><br>
<a name="5.5.6">All comfort, joy, in this most gracious lady,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.7">Heaven ever laid up to make parents happy,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.8">May hourly fall upon ye!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.5.9">Thank you, good lord archbishop:</a><br>
<a name="5.5.10">What is her name?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.5.11">                  Elizabeth.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.5.12">Stand up, lord.</a><br>
<p><i>KING HENRY VIII kisses the child</i></p>
<a name="5.5.13">With this kiss take my blessing: God protect thee!</a><br>
<a name="5.5.14">Into whose hand I give thy life.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.5.15">Amen.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.5.16">My noble gossips, ye have been too prodigal:</a><br>
<a name="5.5.17">I thank ye heartily; so shall this lady,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.18">When she has so much English.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.5.19">Let me speak, sir,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.20">For heaven now bids me; and the words I utter</a><br>
<a name="5.5.21">Let none think flattery, for they'll find 'em truth.</a><br>
<a name="5.5.22">This royal infant--heaven still move about her!--</a><br>
<a name="5.5.23">Though in her cradle, yet now promises</a><br>
<a name="5.5.24">Upon this land a thousand thousand blessings,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.25">Which time shall bring to ripeness: she shall be--</a><br>
<a name="5.5.26">But few now living can behold that goodness--</a><br>
<a name="5.5.27">A pattern to all princes living with her,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.28">And all that shall succeed: Saba was never</a><br>
<a name="5.5.29">More covetous of wisdom and fair virtue</a><br>
<a name="5.5.30">Than this pure soul shall be: all princely graces,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.31">That mould up such a mighty piece as this is,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.32">With all the virtues that attend the good,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.33">Shall still be doubled on her: truth shall nurse her,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.34">Holy and heavenly thoughts still counsel her:</a><br>
<a name="5.5.35">She shall be loved and fear'd: her own shall bless her;</a><br>
<a name="5.5.36">Her foes shake like a field of beaten corn,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.37">And hang their heads with sorrow: good grows with her:</a><br>
<a name="5.5.38">In her days every man shall eat in safety,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.39">Under his own vine, what he plants; and sing</a><br>
<a name="5.5.40">The merry songs of peace to all his neighbours:</a><br>
<a name="5.5.41">God shall be truly known; and those about her</a><br>
<a name="5.5.42">From her shall read the perfect ways of honour,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.43">And by those claim their greatness, not by blood.</a><br>
<a name="5.5.44">Nor shall this peace sleep with her: but as when</a><br>
<a name="5.5.45">The bird of wonder dies, the maiden phoenix,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.46">Her ashes new create another heir,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.47">As great in admiration as herself;</a><br>
<a name="5.5.48">So shall she leave her blessedness to one,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.49">When heaven shall call her from this cloud of darkness,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.50">Who from the sacred ashes of her honour</a><br>
<a name="5.5.51">Shall star-like rise, as great in fame as she was,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.52">And so stand fix'd: peace, plenty, love, truth, terror,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.53">That were the servants to this chosen infant,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.54">Shall then be his, and like a vine grow to him:</a><br>
<a name="5.5.55">Wherever the bright sun of heaven shall shine,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.56">His honour and the greatness of his name</a><br>
<a name="5.5.57">Shall be, and make new nations: he shall flourish,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.58">And, like a mountain cedar, reach his branches</a><br>
<a name="5.5.59">To all the plains about him: our children's children</a><br>
<a name="5.5.60">Shall see this, and bless heaven.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.5.61">Thou speakest wonders.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>CRANMER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.5.62">She shall be, to the happiness of England,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.63">An aged princess; many days shall see her,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.64">And yet no day without a deed to crown it.</a><br>
<a name="5.5.65">Would I had known no more! but she must die,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.66">She must, the saints must have her; yet a virgin,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.67">A most unspotted lily shall she pass</a><br>
<a name="5.5.68">To the ground, and all the world shall mourn her.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>KING HENRY VIII</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.5.69">O lord archbishop,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.70">Thou hast made me now a man! never, before</a><br>
<a name="5.5.71">This happy child, did I get any thing:</a><br>
<a name="5.5.72">This oracle of comfort has so pleased me,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.73">That when I am in heaven I shall desire</a><br>
<a name="5.5.74">To see what this child does, and praise my Maker.</a><br>
<a name="5.5.75">I thank ye all. To you, my good lord mayor,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.76">And your good brethren, I am much beholding;</a><br>
<a name="5.5.77">I have received much honour by your presence,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.78">And ye shall find me thankful. Lead the way, lords:</a><br>
<a name="5.5.79">Ye must all see the queen, and she must thank ye,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.80">She will be sick else. This day, no man think</a><br>
<a name="5.5.81">Has business at his house; for all shall stay:</a><br>
<a name="5.5.82">This little one shall make it holiday.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
<a name="5.5.83">EPILOGUE</a><br>
<a name="5.5.84">'Tis ten to one this play can never please</a><br>
<a name="5.5.85">All that are here: some come to take their ease,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.86">And sleep an act or two; but those, we fear,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.87">We have frighted with our trumpets; so, 'tis clear,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.88">They'll say 'tis naught: others, to hear the city</a><br>
<a name="5.5.89">Abused extremely, and to cry 'That's witty!'</a><br>
<a name="5.5.90">Which we have not done neither: that, I fear,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.91">All the expected good we're like to hear</a><br>
<a name="5.5.92">For this play at this time, is only in</a><br>
<a name="5.5.93">The merciful construction of good women;</a><br>
<a name="5.5.94">For such a one we show'd 'em: if they smile,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.95">And say 'twill do, I know, within a while</a><br>
<a name="5.5.96">All the best men are ours; for 'tis ill hap,</a><br>
<a name="5.5.97">If they hold when their ladies bid 'em clap.</a><br>




</blockquote>
